      <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="cmp-image__popup">
        
          <img src="/site/en/images/blog/2024/the-definitive-guide-to-linux-process-injection-twelve.jpg" loading="lazy" class="cmp-image__image" itemprop="contentUrl" data-cmp-hook-image="image" alt="When the function finishes execution, the processor takes this return address from the stack and jumps to it (Figure 12). "/>
        
        
      </a>
    

    <span class="cmp-image__title" itemprop="caption">Fig. 12: Return address on the stack pointing to an address in main</span>
    
    
  </div>
</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-9bfe871e5f&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T01:50:23Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>To abuse this mechanism, we can identify a return address on the stack and overwrite it with a new address that points to our shellcode. As soon as the current function finishes execution, our code will run (Figure 13).&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>To abuse this mechanism, we can identify a return address on the stack and overwrite it with a new address that points to our shellcode. As soon as the current function finishes execution, our code will run (Figure 13).</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="image aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div class="cmp-image__wrapper  cmp-image-popup  ">
  <div data-cmp-lazy data-title="To abuse this mechanism, we can identify a return address on the stack and overwrite it with a new address that points to our shellcode. As soon as the current function finishes execution, our code will run (Figure 13). " class="cmp-image " itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;image-c153cb6de8&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/image&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T01:51:14Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;To abuse this mechanism, we can identify a return address on the stack and overwrite it with a new address that points to our shellcode. As soon as the current function finishes execution, our code will run (Figure 13).&#34;,&#34;image&#34;:{&#34;repo:id&#34;:&#34;30ad1ec7-f6cf-4e89-bf78-a47675b14704&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T01:51:04Z&#34;,&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;image/jpeg&#34;,&#34;repo:path&#34;:&#34;/content/dam/site/en/images/blog/2024/the-definitive-guide-to-linux-process-injection-thirteen.jpg&#34;}}}">
    
    
      <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="cmp-image__popup">
        
          <img src="/site/en/images/blog/2024/the-definitive-guide-to-linux-process-injection-thirteen.jpg" loading="lazy" class="cmp-image__image" itemprop="contentUrl" data-cmp-hook-image="image" alt="To abuse this mechanism, we can identify a return address on the stack and overwrite it with a new address that points to our shellcode. As soon as the current function finishes execution, our code will run (Figure 13). "/>
        
        
      </a>
    

    <span class="cmp-image__title" itemprop="caption">Fig. 13: Overwriting a return address on the stack to point to the attackers code</span>
    
    
  </div>
</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-17e3c136fd&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T01:52:35Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>To identify the top of the stack we can parse the previously mentioned procfs syscall file, which also contains the value of the stack pointer register.&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>The following six steps can be used to perform this technique:&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;ol>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>Stop the process execution by sending a SIGSTOP signal&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>Identify the stack pointer of the process by parsing the procfs syscall file&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>Scan the process stack and identify a return address&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>Use any of the previously mentioned write primitives to inject our payload to the process memory&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>Overwrite the return address with the address of our payload&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>Resume process execution by sending a SIGCONT signal&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;/ol>\r\n&lt;p>When the current function finishes execution, our payload is executed.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>As all the process interaction methods allow us to modify the stack, they all can be used to implement this technique. Our implementation of this technique using the process_vm_writev syscall can be &lt;a href=\&#34;https://github.com/guardicode/linux_injection_examples/blob/main/src/proc_vm_writev_primitives.c#L39\&#34; target=\&#34;_blank\&#34; rel=\&#34;nofollow noreferrer\&#34;>found in our repository&lt;/a>.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>To identify the top of the stack we can parse the previously mentioned procfs syscall file, which also contains the value of the stack pointer register. </p>
<p>The following six steps can be used to perform this technique:</p>
<ol>
<li><p>Stop the process execution by sending a SIGSTOP signal</p>
</li>
<li><p>Identify the stack pointer of the process by parsing the procfs syscall file</p>
</li>
<li><p>Scan the process stack and identify a return address</p>
</li>
<li><p>Use any of the previously mentioned write primitives to inject our payload to the process memory</p>
</li>
<li><p>Overwrite the return address with the address of our payload</p>
</li>
<li><p>Resume process execution by sending a SIGCONT signal</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>When the current function finishes execution, our payload is executed.</p>
<p>As all the process interaction methods allow us to modify the stack, they all can be used to implement this technique. Our implementation of this technique using the process_vm_writev syscall can be <a href="https://github.com/guardicode/linux_injection_examples/blob/main/src/proc_vm_writev_primitives.c#L39" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer">found in our repository</a>.</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="title title__paddingBottom-none aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div class="cmp-title" id="title-4a8bed870f" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;title-4a8bed870f&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/title&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T01:53:16Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;ROP stack hijacking&lt;br>\r\n&#34;}}">
    
      <h3 class="cmp-title__text">ROP stack hijacking<br />
</h3>
    
</div>

    


</div>
<div class="text text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-6b78178f5d&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T01:54:00Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>&lt;b>Applicable to&lt;/b>: ptrace, procfs mem file, process_vm_writev&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>The stack hijacking technique is interesting in that it allows us to hijack the execution flow of the process without modifying any executable memory or registers. Despite that, for it to be usable, we still need to jump to shellcode that resides in an executable memory region. We can attempt to find a WX region (as we’ve described) or use ptrace/procfs mem to write to non-writable memory.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>But what if we want to avoid these actions? Well, we have another trick up our sleeve — &lt;a href=\&#34;https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Return-oriented_programming\&#34; target=\&#34;_blank\&#34; rel=\&#34;nofollow noreferrer\&#34;>return-oriented programming&lt;/a> (ROP). By using our ability to write to the process stack, we can overwrite it with a ROP chain (Figure 14). As we rely on executable gadgets that already reside in the process memory, we can construct a payload without writing any new executable code.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p><b>Applicable to</b>: ptrace, procfs mem file, process_vm_writev</p>
<p>The stack hijacking technique is interesting in that it allows us to hijack the execution flow of the process without modifying any executable memory or registers. Despite that, for it to be usable, we still need to jump to shellcode that resides in an executable memory region. We can attempt to find a WX region (as we’ve described) or use ptrace/procfs mem to write to non-writable memory.</p>
<p>But what if we want to avoid these actions? Well, we have another trick up our sleeve — <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Return-oriented_programming" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer">return-oriented programming</a> (ROP). By using our ability to write to the process stack, we can overwrite it with a ROP chain (Figure 14). As we rely on executable gadgets that already reside in the process memory, we can construct a payload without writing any new executable code.</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="image aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div class="cmp-image__wrapper  cmp-image-popup  ">
  <div data-cmp-lazy data-title="But what if we want to avoid these actions? Well, we have another trick up our sleeve — return-oriented programming (ROP). By using our ability to write to the process stack, we can overwrite it with a ROP chain (Figure 14). " class="cmp-image " itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;image-6b1b9f7cbb&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/image&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T01:59:41Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;But what if we want to avoid these actions? Well, we have another trick up our sleeve — return-oriented programming (ROP). By using our ability to write to the process stack, we can overwrite it with a ROP chain (Figure 14).&#34;,&#34;image&#34;:{&#34;repo:id&#34;:&#34;aeba47ed-381f-4d4a-9e1e-ba1628784190&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T01:55:06Z&#34;,&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;image/jpeg&#34;,&#34;repo:path&#34;:&#34;/content/dam/site/en/images/blog/2024/the-definitive-guide-to-linux-process-injection-fourteen.jpg&#34;}}}">
    
    
      <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="cmp-image__popup">
        
          <img src="/site/en/images/blog/2024/the-definitive-guide-to-linux-process-injection-fourteen.jpg" loading="lazy" class="cmp-image__image" itemprop="contentUrl" data-cmp-hook-image="image" alt="But what if we want to avoid these actions? Well, we have another trick up our sleeve — return-oriented programming (ROP). By using our ability to write to the process stack, we can overwrite it with a ROP chain (Figure 14). "/>
        
        
      </a>
    

    <span class="cmp-image__title" itemprop="caption">Fig. 14: Injecting a ROP chain to the process stack</span>
    
    
  </div>
</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-8d74969a93&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T02:01:14Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>&amp;nbsp;This technique will consist of the following seven steps:&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;ol>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>Stop the process execution by sending a SIGSTOP signal&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>Identify the stack pointer of the process by parsing the procfs syscall file&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>Scan the process stack and identify a return address&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>Use any of the previously mentioned write primitives to inject our payload to a writable memory region without execution permissions&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>Construct a ROP chain to call &lt;a href=\&#34;https://man7.org/linux/man-pages/man2/mprotect.2.html\&#34; target=\&#34;_blank\&#34; rel=\&#34;nofollow noreferrer\&#34;>&lt;i>mprotect&lt;/i>&lt;/a> and mark the memory region of our shellcode executable&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>Overwrite the stack with the ROP chain, starting at the address of the identified return address&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>Resume process execution by sending a SIGCONT signal&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;/ol>\r\n&lt;p>When the current function finishes execution, our ROP chain executes, making the shellcode executable and jumping to it.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>This concept was demonstrated by Rory McNamara of AON Cyber Labs in his &lt;a href=\&#34;https://www.aon.com/cyber-solutions/aon_cyber_labs/linux-based-inter-process-code-injection-without-ptrace2/\&#34; target=\&#34;_blank\&#34; rel=\&#34;nofollow noreferrer\&#34;>blog post&lt;/a> that covers procfs mem injection.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>This technique doesn’t require modifying any non-writable memory regions, and can therefore be performed using all of the process interaction techniques, including process_vm_writev.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>&lt;a href=\&#34;https://github.com/guardicode/linux_injection_examples/blob/main/src/proc_vm_writev_primitives.c#L71\&#34; target=\&#34;_blank\&#34; rel=\&#34;nofollow noreferrer\&#34;>Check out&lt;/a> our implementation of this technique using process_vm_writev. As far as we are aware, this is the first public demonstration of an injection technique that relies only on the process_vm_writev syscall.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p> This technique will consist of the following seven steps:</p>
<ol>
<li><p>Stop the process execution by sending a SIGSTOP signal</p>
</li>
<li><p>Identify the stack pointer of the process by parsing the procfs syscall file</p>
</li>
<li><p>Scan the process stack and identify a return address</p>
</li>
<li><p>Use any of the previously mentioned write primitives to inject our payload to a writable memory region without execution permissions</p>
</li>
<li><p>Construct a ROP chain to call <a href="https://man7.org/linux/man-pages/man2/mprotect.2.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer"><i>mprotect</i></a> and mark the memory region of our shellcode executable</p>
</li>
<li><p>Overwrite the stack with the ROP chain, starting at the address of the identified return address</p>
</li>
<li><p>Resume process execution by sending a SIGCONT signal</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>When the current function finishes execution, our ROP chain executes, making the shellcode executable and jumping to it.</p>
<p>This concept was demonstrated by Rory McNamara of AON Cyber Labs in his <a href="https://www.aon.com/cyber-solutions/aon_cyber_labs/linux-based-inter-process-code-injection-without-ptrace2/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer">blog post</a> that covers procfs mem injection.</p>
<p>This technique doesn’t require modifying any non-writable memory regions, and can therefore be performed using all of the process interaction techniques, including process_vm_writev.</p>
<p><a href="https://github.com/guardicode/linux_injection_examples/blob/main/src/proc_vm_writev_primitives.c#L71" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer">Check out</a> our implementation of this technique using process_vm_writev. As far as we are aware, this is the first public demonstration of an injection technique that relies only on the process_vm_writev syscall.</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="title title__paddingBottom-none aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div class="cmp-title" id="title-ef364be7c0" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;title-ef364be7c0&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/title&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T02:01:55Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;GOT hijacking&lt;br>\r\n&#34;}}">
    
      <h3 class="cmp-title__text">GOT hijacking<br />
</h3>
    
</div>

    


</div>
<div class="text aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-fdea6e246a&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T02:02:22Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>&lt;b>Applicable to&lt;/b>: ptrace, procfs mem file, process_vm_writev&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>Another interesting memory section that will usually be writable is the GOT. The Global Offset Table (GOT) is a memory section used as part of the relocation process of dynamically linked ELF files. We will not go into the full details here, but rather focus on the part that is relevant for our purpose — the section that stores addresses of functions imported by the program. Whenever the program calls a function from a remote library, it resolves its memory address by accessing the GOT (Figure 15).&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p><b>Applicable to</b>: ptrace, procfs mem file, process_vm_writev</p>
<p>Another interesting memory section that will usually be writable is the GOT. The Global Offset Table (GOT) is a memory section used as part of the relocation process of dynamically linked ELF files. We will not go into the full details here, but rather focus on the part that is relevant for our purpose — the section that stores addresses of functions imported by the program. Whenever the program calls a function from a remote library, it resolves its memory address by accessing the GOT (Figure 15).</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="image aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div class="cmp-image__wrapper  cmp-image-popup  ">
  <div data-cmp-lazy data-title="Whenever the program calls a function from a remote library, it resolves its memory address by accessing the GOT (Figure 15)." class="cmp-image " itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;image-c03a5a72e1&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/image&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T02:05:20Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;Whenever the program calls a function from a remote library, it resolves its memory address by accessing the GOT (Figure 15).&#34;,&#34;image&#34;:{&#34;repo:id&#34;:&#34;380a80ad-c8be-4ea2-ae13-b35cd72ce645&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T02:05:09Z&#34;,&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;image/jpeg&#34;,&#34;repo:path&#34;:&#34;/content/dam/site/en/images/blog/2024/the-definitive-guide-to-linux-process-injection-fifteen.jpg&#34;}}}">
    
    
      <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="cmp-image__popup">
        
          <img src="/site/en/images/blog/2024/the-definitive-guide-to-linux-process-injection-fifteen.jpg" loading="lazy" class="cmp-image__image" itemprop="contentUrl" data-cmp-hook-image="image" alt="Whenever the program calls a function from a remote library, it resolves its memory address by accessing the GOT (Figure 15)."/>
        
        
      </a>
    

    <span class="cmp-image__title" itemprop="caption">Fig. 15: Resolving a library function address using the GOT</span>
    
    
  </div>
</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-9318527951&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T02:03:36Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>This mechanism can be abused by an attacker to hijack the process execution flow. The GOT memory is normally writable, meaning that an attacker can overwrite any of the addresses inside it with the address of their payload. The next time the function is called by the process, the attacker code will execute instead (Figure 16).&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>This mechanism can be abused by an attacker to hijack the process execution flow. The GOT memory is normally writable, meaning that an attacker can overwrite any of the addresses inside it with the address of their payload. The next time the function is called by the process, the attacker code will execute instead (Figure 16).</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="image aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div class="cmp-image__wrapper  cmp-image-popup  ">
  <div data-cmp-lazy data-title=" The GOT memory is normally writable, meaning that an attacker can overwrite any of the addresses inside it with the address of their payload. The next time the function is called by the process, the attacker code will execute instead (Figure 16)." class="cmp-image " itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;image-177ec43dac&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/image&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T02:06:41Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;The GOT memory is normally writable, meaning that an attacker can overwrite any of the addresses inside it with the address of their payload. The next time the function is called by the process, the attacker code will execute instead (Figure 16).&#34;,&#34;image&#34;:{&#34;repo:id&#34;:&#34;a03d9d43-0bd3-40bc-8f52-6b2a5a65bb64&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T02:06:26Z&#34;,&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;image/jpeg&#34;,&#34;repo:path&#34;:&#34;/content/dam/site/en/images/blog/2024/the-definitive-guide-to-linux-process-injection-sixteen.jpg&#34;}}}">
    
    
      <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="cmp-image__popup">
        
          <img src="/site/en/images/blog/2024/the-definitive-guide-to-linux-process-injection-sixteen.jpg" loading="lazy" class="cmp-image__image" itemprop="contentUrl" data-cmp-hook-image="image" alt=" The GOT memory is normally writable, meaning that an attacker can overwrite any of the addresses inside it with the address of their payload. The next time the function is called by the process, the attacker code will execute instead (Figure 16)."/>
        
        
      </a>
    

    <span class="cmp-image__title" itemprop="caption">Fig. 16: Modifying a function in the GOT to point to the attacker payload</span>
    
    
  </div>
</div>

    

</div>
<div class="text aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-760af9eb4e&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T02:07:52Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>This technique will consist of the following four steps:&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;ol>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>Stop the process execution by sending a SIGSTOP signal&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>Identify the GOT memory region by parsing the maps file&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>Overwrite addresses in the section with the address of our payload&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>Resume process execution by sending a SIGCONT signal&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;/ol>\r\n&lt;p>&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>When any of our overwritten functions are called, our payload executes.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>One memory protection that might affect this attack is &lt;a href=\&#34;https://ctf101.org/binary-exploitation/relocation-read-only/\&#34; target=\&#34;_blank\&#34; rel=\&#34;nofollow noreferrer\&#34;>full RELRO&lt;/a>; compiling a binary with this setting will cause the GOT memory to have read-only permissions and potentially prevent overwrites.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>Despite that, RELRO will not be able to prevent this attack in most cases.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;ul>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>ptrace and procfs mem bypass memory permissions, making RELRO irrelevant&amp;nbsp;&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>RELRO affects the process binary itself but not its loaded libraries. If the process loads any library that was compiled without RELRO, its GOT will be writable, enabling us to overwrite it&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;/ul>\r\n&lt;p>Our implementation of this technique using the process_vm_writev syscall can be &lt;a href=\&#34;https://github.com/guardicode/linux_injection_examples/blob/main/src/proc_vm_writev_primitives.c#L157\&#34; target=\&#34;_blank\&#34; rel=\&#34;nofollow noreferrer\&#34;>found in our repository&lt;/a>.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>This technique will consist of the following four steps:</p>
<ol>
<li><p>Stop the process execution by sending a SIGSTOP signal</p>
</li>
<li><p>Identify the GOT memory region by parsing the maps file</p>
</li>
<li><p>Overwrite addresses in the section with the address of our payload</p>
</li>
<li><p>Resume process execution by sending a SIGCONT signal</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p> </p>
<p>When any of our overwritten functions are called, our payload executes.</p>
<p>One memory protection that might affect this attack is <a href="https://ctf101.org/binary-exploitation/relocation-read-only/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer">full RELRO</a>; compiling a binary with this setting will cause the GOT memory to have read-only permissions and potentially prevent overwrites.</p>
<p>Despite that, RELRO will not be able to prevent this attack in most cases.</p>
<ul>
<li><p>ptrace and procfs mem bypass memory permissions, making RELRO irrelevant </p>
</li>
<li><p>RELRO affects the process binary itself but not its loaded libraries. If the process loads any library that was compiled without RELRO, its GOT will be writable, enabling us to overwrite it</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Our implementation of this technique using the process_vm_writev syscall can be <a href="https://github.com/guardicode/linux_injection_examples/blob/main/src/proc_vm_writev_primitives.c#L157" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer">found in our repository</a>.</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="title title__paddingBottom-none aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div class="cmp-title" id="title-a448a1d8f2" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;title-a448a1d8f2&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/title&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T02:08:19Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;Execution primitives summary&lt;br>\r\n&#34;}}">
    
      <h2 class="cmp-title__text">Execution primitives summary<br />
</h2>
    
</div>

    


</div>
<div class="text aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-69eac62c9e&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T02:08:33Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>The table summarizes all the possible execution primitives that we described, and with which methods they could be implemented.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>The table summarizes all the possible execution primitives that we described, and with which methods they could be implemented.</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="image aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div class="cmp-image__wrapper  cmp-image-popup  ">
  <div data-cmp-lazy data-title="The table summarizes all the possible execution primitives that we described, and with which methods they could be implemented." class="cmp-image " itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;image-d44b608e10&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/image&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T02:09:44Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;The table summarizes all the possible execution primitives that we described, and with which methods they could be implemented.&#34;,&#34;image&#34;:{&#34;repo:id&#34;:&#34;b49bf423-6b16-4a2b-ab79-d91ef9ba71ac&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T02:09:30Z&#34;,&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;image/jpeg&#34;,&#34;repo:path&#34;:&#34;/content/dam/site/en/images/blog/2024/the-definitive-guide-to-linux-process-injection-seventeen.jpg&#34;}}}">
    
    
      <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="cmp-image__popup">
        
          <img src="/site/en/images/blog/2024/the-definitive-guide-to-linux-process-injection-seventeen.jpg" loading="lazy" class="cmp-image__image" itemprop="contentUrl" data-cmp-hook-image="image" alt="The table summarizes all the possible execution primitives that we described, and with which methods they could be implemented."/>
        
        
      </a>
    

    <span class="cmp-image__title" itemprop="caption">All the possible execution primitives and the methods that could be used to implement them</span>
    
    
  </div>
</div>

    

</div>
<div class="title title__paddingBottom-none aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div class="cmp-title" id="title-fc97968a18" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;title-fc97968a18&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/title&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T02:10:03Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;Limitations on remote process interaction&lt;br>\r\n&#34;}}">
    
      <h2 class="cmp-title__text">Limitations on remote process interaction<br />
</h2>
    
</div>

    


</div>
<div class="text aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-28d7beb397&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T02:10:21Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>There are multiple settings that will determine our ability to interact with remote processes using the methods we just described. In this section, we will briefly cover the two main ones.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>There are multiple settings that will determine our ability to interact with remote processes using the methods we just described. In this section, we will briefly cover the two main ones.</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="title title__paddingBottom-none aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div class="cmp-title" id="title-4e2f72955e" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;title-4e2f72955e&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/title&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T02:10:38Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;ptrace_scope&lt;br>\r\n&#34;}}">
    
      <h3 class="cmp-title__text">ptrace_scope<br />
</h3>
    
</div>

    


</div>
<div class="text aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-ea7298ef8d&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T02:11:28Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>&lt;a href=\&#34;https://www.kernel.org/doc/Documentation/security/Yama.txt\&#34; target=\&#34;_blank\&#34; rel=\&#34;nofollow noreferrer\&#34;>ptrace_scope&lt;/a> is a setting that determines who is allowed to use ptrace on remote processes. It can have the following values:&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>0 — Processes can attach to any other process on the system, as long as it has the same UID.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>1 — Normal processes can only attach to their child processes. Privileged processes (with &lt;a href=\&#34;https://man7.org/linux/man-pages/man7/capabilities.7.html#:~:text=acct(2).-,CAP_SYS_PTRACE,-%E2%80%A2%20%20Trace%20arbitrary%20processes\&#34; target=\&#34;_blank\&#34; rel=\&#34;nofollow noreferrer\&#34;>CAP_SYS_PTRACE&lt;/a>) can still attach to unrelated processes. This is the default setting in many distributions.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>2 — Only processes with CAP_SYS_PTRACE are able to attach to processes. This capability is usually granted only to root.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>3 — Attaching to remote processes is disabled.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>Despite its name, this setting will also affect the ability to access the procfs mem file of remote processes, and to use process_vm_writev on them.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p><a href="https://www.kernel.org/doc/Documentation/security/Yama.txt" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer">ptrace_scope</a> is a setting that determines who is allowed to use ptrace on remote processes. It can have the following values:</p>
<p>0 — Processes can attach to any other process on the system, as long as it has the same UID.</p>
<p>1 — Normal processes can only attach to their child processes. Privileged processes (with <a href="https://man7.org/linux/man-pages/man7/capabilities.7.html#:~:text=acct(2).-,CAP_SYS_PTRACE,-%E2%80%A2%20%20Trace%20arbitrary%20processes" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer">CAP_SYS_PTRACE</a>) can still attach to unrelated processes. This is the default setting in many distributions.</p>
<p>2 — Only processes with CAP_SYS_PTRACE are able to attach to processes. This capability is usually granted only to root.</p>
<p>3 — Attaching to remote processes is disabled.</p>
<p>Despite its name, this setting will also affect the ability to access the procfs mem file of remote processes, and to use process_vm_writev on them.</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="title title__paddingBottom-none aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div class="cmp-title" id="title-d47dab59d2" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;title-d47dab59d2&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/title&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T02:11:45Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;The “dumpable” attribute&lt;br>\r\n&#34;}}">
    
      <h3 class="cmp-title__text">The “dumpable” attribute<br />
</h3>
    
</div>

    


</div>
<div class="text aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-b23fac223c&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T02:12:29Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>Every process in Linux is configured with the “&lt;a href=\&#34;https://man7.org/linux/man-pages/man2/prctl.2.html#:~:text=int%20*)%20arg2.-,PR_SET_DUMPABLE,-(since%20Linux%202.3.20\&#34; target=\&#34;_blank\&#34; rel=\&#34;nofollow noreferrer\&#34;>dumpable&lt;/a>” attribute, which is set to true by default. A process will become undumpable automatically under &lt;a href=\&#34;https://man7.org/linux/man-pages/man2/prctl.2.html#:~:text=Normally%2C%20the%20%22dumpable,for%20the%20process.\&#34; target=\&#34;_blank\&#34; rel=\&#34;nofollow noreferrer\&#34;>certain circumstances&lt;/a>, or configured as such manually by calling &lt;a href=\&#34;https://man7.org/linux/man-pages/man2/prctl.2.html#:~:text=int%20*)%20arg2.-,PR_SET_DUMPABLE,-(since%20Linux%202.3.20\&#34; target=\&#34;_blank\&#34; rel=\&#34;nofollow noreferrer\&#34;>prctl&lt;/a>.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>If a process is not dumpable, we will not be able to access it remotely with any of the previously mentioned methods. This setting will override other ones — an un-dumpable process cannot be remotely modified.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>Every process in Linux is configured with the “<a href="https://man7.org/linux/man-pages/man2/prctl.2.html#:~:text=int%20*)%20arg2.-,PR_SET_DUMPABLE,-(since%20Linux%202.3.20" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer">dumpable</a>” attribute, which is set to true by default. A process will become undumpable automatically under <a href="https://man7.org/linux/man-pages/man2/prctl.2.html#:~:text=Normally%2C%20the%20%22dumpable,for%20the%20process." target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer">certain circumstances</a>, or configured as such manually by calling <a href="https://man7.org/linux/man-pages/man2/prctl.2.html#:~:text=int%20*)%20arg2.-,PR_SET_DUMPABLE,-(since%20Linux%202.3.20" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer">prctl</a>.</p>
<p>If a process is not dumpable, we will not be able to access it remotely with any of the previously mentioned methods. This setting will override other ones — an un-dumpable process cannot be remotely modified.</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="title title__paddingBottom-none aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div class="cmp-title" id="title-857ba7dd54" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;title-857ba7dd54&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/title&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T02:12:55Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;A note on process recovery&lt;br>\r\n&#34;}}">
    
      <h2 class="cmp-title__text">A note on process recovery<br />
</h2>
    
</div>

    


</div>
<div class="text aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-f6eda1e163&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T02:13:39Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>All the injection methods that we highlighted require modifying the process state in some manner — modifying the process registers, or overwriting executable memory, a return address on the stack, or the GOT. All these actions will alter the normal execution flow of the process and will lead to unexpected behavior after our payload finishes.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>This can be problematic when we want the target process to continue running alongside our injected payload. To make sure the process continues to run normally, we will need to restore its original state. The general recovery flow will consist of the following eight steps:&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;ol>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>Back up the memory content that we intend to overwrite by using a remote read primitive&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>Back up the current content of the process registers; this could be performed using ptrace or by our shellcode&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>Execute our payload (e.g., load a shared object (SO) file that runs code in a separate thread)&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>When our payload completes, indicate to the injecting process that execution is finished; this could be implemented by raising an interrupt&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>Pause the remote process&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>Restore the process register state&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>Restore overwritten memory&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>Resume the process execution&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;/ol>\r\n&lt;p>The implementation details will vary slightly depending on the injection method used, but this general outline should be followed. Adam Chester’s &lt;a href=\&#34;https://blog.xpnsec.com/linux-process-injection-aka-injecting-into-sshd-for-fun/\&#34; target=\&#34;_blank\&#34; rel=\&#34;nofollow noreferrer\&#34;>Linux ptrace injection blog post&lt;/a> provides a detailed example of process recovery after a ptrace-based injection.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>Our goal with this post was to provide an overview of injection techniques, which defenders can use to familiarize themselves with the techniques and then build appropriate detection. As our focus is on defense, we chose to not detail the recovery steps for the different techniques, which attackers need to fully weaponize them.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>All the injection methods that we highlighted require modifying the process state in some manner — modifying the process registers, or overwriting executable memory, a return address on the stack, or the GOT. All these actions will alter the normal execution flow of the process and will lead to unexpected behavior after our payload finishes.</p>
<p>This can be problematic when we want the target process to continue running alongside our injected payload. To make sure the process continues to run normally, we will need to restore its original state. The general recovery flow will consist of the following eight steps:</p>
<ol>
<li><p>Back up the memory content that we intend to overwrite by using a remote read primitive</p>
</li>
<li><p>Back up the current content of the process registers; this could be performed using ptrace or by our shellcode</p>
</li>
<li><p>Execute our payload (e.g., load a shared object (SO) file that runs code in a separate thread)</p>
</li>
<li><p>When our payload completes, indicate to the injecting process that execution is finished; this could be implemented by raising an interrupt</p>
</li>
<li><p>Pause the remote process</p>
</li>
<li><p>Restore the process register state</p>
</li>
<li><p>Restore overwritten memory</p>
</li>
<li><p>Resume the process execution</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>The implementation details will vary slightly depending on the injection method used, but this general outline should be followed. Adam Chester’s <a href="https://blog.xpnsec.com/linux-process-injection-aka-injecting-into-sshd-for-fun/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer">Linux ptrace injection blog post</a> provides a detailed example of process recovery after a ptrace-based injection.</p>
<p>Our goal with this post was to provide an overview of injection techniques, which defenders can use to familiarize themselves with the techniques and then build appropriate detection. As our focus is on defense, we chose to not detail the recovery steps for the different techniques, which attackers need to fully weaponize them.</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="title title__paddingBottom-none aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div class="cmp-title" id="title-50c2f071a5" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;title-50c2f071a5&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/title&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T02:18:49Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;Detection and mitigation&lt;br>\r\n&#34;}}">
    
      <h2 class="cmp-title__text">Detection and mitigation<br />
</h2>
    
</div>

    


</div>
<div class="text aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-7aefa6960f&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T02:19:21Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>As we just discussed, there are plenty of techniques that enable attackers to perform process injection on Linux machines. Luckily for us, all these methods require performing anomalous actions that provide detection opportunities. The next sections will detail the different strategies that could be implemented to detect and mitigate process injection on Linux.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>As we just discussed, there are plenty of techniques that enable attackers to perform process injection on Linux machines. Luckily for us, all these methods require performing anomalous actions that provide detection opportunities. The next sections will detail the different strategies that could be implemented to detect and mitigate process injection on Linux.</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="title title__paddingBottom-none aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div class="cmp-title" id="title-f73836889d" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;title-f73836889d&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/title&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T02:19:55Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;“Injection syscalls”&lt;br />\r\n&#34;}}">
    
      <h3 class="cmp-title__text">“Injection syscalls”<br />
</h3>
    
</div>

    


</div>
<div class="text aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-f0981c7257&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T02:21:23Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>Throughout this post, we used three methods to interact with remote processes: ptrace, procfs, and process_vm_writev. Because of their potential for malicious use, these methods should be monitored.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>Start by installing a logging solution on Linux machines. Syscall execution monitoring can be enabled by using an eBPF-based logging utility such as &lt;a href=\&#34;https://github.com/Sysinternals/SysmonForLinux\&#34; target=\&#34;_blank\&#34; rel=\&#34;nofollow noreferrer\&#34;>Sysmon for Linux&lt;/a> or Aqua Security’s &lt;a href=\&#34;https://github.com/aquasecurity/tracee\&#34; target=\&#34;_blank\&#34; rel=\&#34;nofollow noreferrer\&#34;>Tracee&lt;/a> (that already &lt;a href=\&#34;https://aquasecurity.github.io/tracee/v0.19/docs/events/builtin/signatures/ptrace_code_injection/\&#34; target=\&#34;_blank\&#34; rel=\&#34;nofollow noreferrer\&#34;>implements rules&lt;/a> that cover many of the techniques described in this post).&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>After establishing logging, we recommend that organizations analyze the normal use of the “injection syscalls” in their environment and build a baseline of known valid use cases. After such a baseline is created, any deviation from it should be investigated to rule out a potential attack. Additional per-syscall considerations are described in the next sections.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>Ideally, use &lt;a href=\&#34;https://www.kernel.org/doc/Documentation/security/Yama.txt\&#34; target=\&#34;_blank\&#34; rel=\&#34;nofollow noreferrer\&#34;>ptrace_scope&lt;/a> when possible to limit the usage of these syscalls or to prevent it entirely.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>Throughout this post, we used three methods to interact with remote processes: ptrace, procfs, and process_vm_writev. Because of their potential for malicious use, these methods should be monitored.</p>
<p>Start by installing a logging solution on Linux machines. Syscall execution monitoring can be enabled by using an eBPF-based logging utility such as <a href="https://github.com/Sysinternals/SysmonForLinux" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer">Sysmon for Linux</a> or Aqua Security’s <a href="https://github.com/aquasecurity/tracee" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer">Tracee</a> (that already <a href="https://aquasecurity.github.io/tracee/v0.19/docs/events/builtin/signatures/ptrace_code_injection/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer">implements rules</a> that cover many of the techniques described in this post).</p>
<p>After establishing logging, we recommend that organizations analyze the normal use of the “injection syscalls” in their environment and build a baseline of known valid use cases. After such a baseline is created, any deviation from it should be investigated to rule out a potential attack. Additional per-syscall considerations are described in the next sections.</p>
<p>Ideally, use <a href="https://www.kernel.org/doc/Documentation/security/Yama.txt" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer">ptrace_scope</a> when possible to limit the usage of these syscalls or to prevent it entirely.</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="title title__paddingBottom-none aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div class="cmp-title" id="title-6595b21a53" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;title-6595b21a53&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/title&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T02:19:55Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;“Injection syscalls”&lt;br />\r\n&#34;}}">
    
      <h3 class="cmp-title__text">“Injection syscalls”<br />
</h3>
    
</div>

    


</div>
<div class="text aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-e04fcf4926&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T02:26:05Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>Throughout this post, we used three methods to interact with remote processes: ptrace, procfs, and process_vm_writev. Because of their potential for malicious use, these methods should be monitored.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>Start by installing a logging solution on Linux machines. Syscall execution monitoring can be enabled by using an eBPF-based logging utility such as &lt;a href=\&#34;https://github.com/Sysinternals/SysmonForLinux\&#34; target=\&#34;_blank\&#34; rel=\&#34;nofollow noreferrer\&#34;>Sysmon for Linux&lt;/a> or Aqua Security’s &lt;a href=\&#34;https://github.com/aquasecurity/tracee\&#34; target=\&#34;_blank\&#34; rel=\&#34;nofollow noreferrer\&#34;>Tracee&lt;/a> (that already &lt;a href=\&#34;https://aquasecurity.github.io/tracee/v0.19/docs/events/builtin/signatures/ptrace_code_injection/\&#34; target=\&#34;_blank\&#34; rel=\&#34;nofollow noreferrer\&#34;>implements rules&lt;/a> that cover many of the techniques described in this post).&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>After establishing logging, we recommend that organizations analyze the normal use of the “injection syscalls” in their environment and build a baseline of known valid use cases. After such a baseline is created, any deviation from it should be investigated to rule out a potential attack. Additional per-syscall considerations are described in the next sections.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>Ideally, use &lt;a href=\&#34;https://www.kernel.org/doc/Documentation/security/Yama.txt\&#34; target=\&#34;_blank\&#34; rel=\&#34;nofollow noreferrer\&#34;>ptrace_scope&lt;/a> when possible to limit the usage of these syscalls or to prevent it entirely.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>Throughout this post, we used three methods to interact with remote processes: ptrace, procfs, and process_vm_writev. Because of their potential for malicious use, these methods should be monitored.</p>
<p>Start by installing a logging solution on Linux machines. Syscall execution monitoring can be enabled by using an eBPF-based logging utility such as <a href="https://github.com/Sysinternals/SysmonForLinux" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer">Sysmon for Linux</a> or Aqua Security’s <a href="https://github.com/aquasecurity/tracee" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer">Tracee</a> (that already <a href="https://aquasecurity.github.io/tracee/v0.19/docs/events/builtin/signatures/ptrace_code_injection/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer">implements rules</a> that cover many of the techniques described in this post).</p>
<p>After establishing logging, we recommend that organizations analyze the normal use of the “injection syscalls” in their environment and build a baseline of known valid use cases. After such a baseline is created, any deviation from it should be investigated to rule out a potential attack. Additional per-syscall considerations are described in the next sections.</p>
<p>Ideally, use <a href="https://www.kernel.org/doc/Documentation/security/Yama.txt" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer">ptrace_scope</a> when possible to limit the usage of these syscalls or to prevent it entirely.</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="title title__paddingBottom-none aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div class="cmp-title" id="title-9c48827ae9" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;title-9c48827ae9&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/title&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T02:26:31Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;ptrace&lt;br>\r\n&#34;}}">
    
      <h4 class="cmp-title__text">ptrace<br />
</h4>
    
</div>

    


</div>
<div class="text aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-9873a69e20&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T02:26:58Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>In most production environments, usage of the ptrace syscall will likely be pretty rare. After establishing a baseline of valid ptrace usage, we recommend analyzing any abnormal ptrace usage.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>The following ptrace requests allow the modification of remote processes, and should be considered highly suspicious:&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;ul>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>POKEDATA/POKETEXT&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>POKEUSER&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;li>&lt;p>SETREGS&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;/li>\r\n&lt;/ul>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>In most production environments, usage of the ptrace syscall will likely be pretty rare. After establishing a baseline of valid ptrace usage, we recommend analyzing any abnormal ptrace usage.</p>
<p>The following ptrace requests allow the modification of remote processes, and should be considered highly suspicious:</p>
<ul>
<li><p>POKEDATA/POKETEXT</p>
</li>
<li><p>POKEUSER</p>
</li>
<li><p>SETREGS</p>
</li>
</ul>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="title title__paddingBottom-none aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div class="cmp-title" id="title-2ddc98898e" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;title-2ddc98898e&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/title&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T02:27:17Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;procfs&lt;br>\r\n&#34;}}">
    
      <h4 class="cmp-title__text">procfs<br />
</h4>
    
</div>

    


</div>
<div class="text aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-00a548b277&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T02:28:06Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>Writing to the procfs mem file has some legitimate use cases, but this behavior will probably not be very common. After building a baseline of valid use cases, we recommend analyzing any abnormal write operations.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>It’s important to also consider the &lt;i>/proc/&amp;lt;pid&amp;gt;/task&lt;/i> procfs directory. This directory exposes information about the different threads of the process. Each thread will have its own procfs directory, which will contain all the major procfs files we covered, including the mem, maps, and syscall files.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>In Figure 17, we can see that reading the syscall file from the /proc/&amp;lt;pid&amp;gt; directory is equivalent of reading from the /proc/&amp;lt;pid&amp;gt;/task/&amp;lt;pid&amp;gt; directory, which represents the main thread of the process.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>Writing to the procfs mem file has some legitimate use cases, but this behavior will probably not be very common. After building a baseline of valid use cases, we recommend analyzing any abnormal write operations.</p>
<p>It’s important to also consider the <i>/proc/&lt;pid&gt;/task</i> procfs directory. This directory exposes information about the different threads of the process. Each thread will have its own procfs directory, which will contain all the major procfs files we covered, including the mem, maps, and syscall files.</p>
<p>In Figure 17, we can see that reading the syscall file from the /proc/&lt;pid&gt; directory is equivalent of reading from the /proc/&lt;pid&gt;/task/&lt;pid&gt; directory, which represents the main thread of the process.</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="image aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div class="cmp-image__wrapper  cmp-image-popup  ">
  <div data-cmp-lazy data-title="In Figure 17, we can see that reading the syscall file from the /proc/&lt;pid> directory is equivalent of reading from the /proc/&lt;pid>/task/&lt;pid> directory, which represents the main thread of the process." class="cmp-image " itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;image-2da5d39e8e&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/image&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T02:52:10Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;In Figure 17, we can see that reading the syscall file from the /proc/&lt;pid> directory is equivalent of reading from the /proc/&lt;pid>/task/&lt;pid> directory, which represents the main thread of the process.&#34;,&#34;image&#34;:{&#34;repo:id&#34;:&#34;28b64da6-a64c-4bc8-904f-ee3e54c302bd&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T02:51:59Z&#34;,&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;image/jpeg&#34;,&#34;repo:path&#34;:&#34;/content/dam/site/en/images/blog/2024/the-definitive-guide-to-linux-process-injection-eigteen.jpg&#34;}}}">
    
    
      <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="cmp-image__popup">
        
          <img src="/site/en/images/blog/2024/the-definitive-guide-to-linux-process-injection-eigteen.jpg" loading="lazy" class="cmp-image__image" itemprop="contentUrl" data-cmp-hook-image="image" alt="In Figure 17, we can see that reading the syscall file from the /proc/&lt;pid> directory is equivalent of reading from the /proc/&lt;pid>/task/&lt;pid> directory, which represents the main thread of the process."/>
        
        
      </a>
    

    <span class="cmp-image__title" itemprop="caption">Fig. 17: Example of using the /proc/&lt;pid&gt;/task directory</span>
    
    
  </div>
</div>

    

</div>
<div class="title title__paddingBottom-none aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div class="cmp-title" id="title-497760faaa" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;title-497760faaa&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/title&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T02:52:30Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;process_vm_writev&lt;br>\r\n&#34;}}">
    
      <h4 class="cmp-title__text">process_vm_writev<br />
</h4>
    
</div>

    


</div>
<div class="text aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-f656f8263f&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T02:52:47Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>Once again, by building a baseline of legitimate uses of this syscall, we can identify anomalous deviations. Any unknown process that writes to the memory of other processes should be considered suspicious and analyzed.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>Once again, by building a baseline of legitimate uses of this syscall, we can identify anomalous deviations. Any unknown process that writes to the memory of other processes should be considered suspicious and analyzed.</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="title title__paddingBottom-none aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div class="cmp-title" id="title-bcd716e202" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;title-bcd716e202&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/title&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T02:53:23Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;Detect process anomalies&lt;br>\r\n&#34;}}">
    
      <h3 class="cmp-title__text">Detect process anomalies<br />
</h3>
    
</div>

    


</div>
<div class="text aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-209fe5716b&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T02:55:33Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>In addition to detecting process injection directly, we can also attempt to detect its side effects. When code is injected to a remote process, it will change the way it behaves. In addition to the normal actions taken by the process, the actions of the payload are now also performed by the same process.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>This change in behavior can provide a detection opportunity. By building a baseline of normal process behavior, we can identify suspicious deviations from it that may indicate that code injection occurred. A few examples of such behaviors can include spawning anomalous child processes, loading of SO files that were previously not seen, or communicating over abnormal ports.&lt;/p>\r\n&lt;p>Akamai researchers &lt;a href=\&#34;/content/akamai/en/blog/security-research/novel-detection-methodology-process-injection-using-network-anomalies.html\&#34; target=\&#34;_self\&#34;>documented this approach&lt;/a> and demonstrated how to identify code injection by analyzing network anomalies.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>In addition to detecting process injection directly, we can also attempt to detect its side effects. When code is injected to a remote process, it will change the way it behaves. In addition to the normal actions taken by the process, the actions of the payload are now also performed by the same process.</p>
<p>This change in behavior can provide a detection opportunity. By building a baseline of normal process behavior, we can identify suspicious deviations from it that may indicate that code injection occurred. A few examples of such behaviors can include spawning anomalous child processes, loading of SO files that were previously not seen, or communicating over abnormal ports.</p>
<p>Akamai researchers <a href="/blog/security-research/novel-detection-methodology-process-injection-using-network-anomalies" target="_self">documented this approach</a> and demonstrated how to identify code injection by analyzing network anomalies.</p>

</div>

    

</div>
<div class="title title__paddingBottom-none aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div class="cmp-title" id="title-bf48891774" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;title-bf48891774&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/title&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T02:55:54Z&#34;,&#34;dc:title&#34;:&#34;Summary&lt;br>\r\n&#34;}}">
    
      <h2 class="cmp-title__text">Summary<br />
</h2>
    
</div>

    


</div>
<div class="text text__pb-20 aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div data-cmp-is="text" class="cmp-text" data-cmp-data-layer="{&#34;text-c6ebebaf61&#34;:{&#34;@type&#34;:&#34;akamai/components/text&#34;,&#34;repo:modifyDate&#34;:&#34;2024-11-13T02:56:11Z&#34;,&#34;xdm:text&#34;:&#34;&lt;p>Attackers have plenty of different options to perform injection attacks on Linux machines. Although these techniques can be very useful for attackers, they also provide valuable detection opportunities for defenders. By implementing solid logging and detection capabilities on Linux machines, organizations can improve their security posture significantly.&lt;/p>\r\n&#34;}}">
    <p>Attackers have plenty of different options to perform injection attacks on Linux machines. Although these techniques can be very useful for attackers, they also provide valuable detection opportunities for defenders. By implementing solid logging and detection capabilities on Linux machines, organizations can improve their security posture significantly.</p>

</div>

    

</div>

<div class="experiencefragment aem-GridColumn aem-GridColumn--default--12">
<div id="experiencefragment-19ce14fea0" class="cmp-experiencefragment cmp-experiencefragment--qualtrics-survey">


    

  
   
      <div data-scroll-to-id="container-d5d7d69b1a" class="cmp-container ">
